<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2018 50:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-01-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The genus Oryza is a model system for the study of molecular evolution over time scales ranging from a few thousand to 15 million years. Using 13 reference genomes spanning the Oryza species tree, we show that despite few large-scale chromosomal rearrangements rapid species diversification is mirrored by lineage-specific emergence and turnover of many novel elements, including transposons, and potential new coding and noncoding genes. Our study resolves controversial areas of the Oryza phylogeny, showing a complex history of introgression among different chromosomes in the young &#8216;AA&#8217; subclade containing the two domesticated species. This study highlights the prevalence of functionally coupled disease resistance genes and identifies many new haplotypes of potential use for future crop protection. Finally, this study marks a milestone in modern rice research with the release of a complete long-read assembly of IR 8 &#8216;Miracle Rice&#8217;, which relieved famine and drove the Green Revolution in Asia 50 years ago. Genome assemblies of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives reveal salient features of genome evolution across the genus Oryza, especially rapid species diversification and turnover of transposons. This study also releases a complete long-read assembly of IR 8 &#8216;Miracle Rice&#8217;."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i> is a model system for the study of molecular evolution over time scales ranging from a few thousand to 15 million years. Using 13 reference genomes spanning the <i>Oryza</i> species tree, we show that despite few large-scale chromosomal rearrangements rapid species diversification is mirrored by lineage-specific emergence and turnover of many novel elements, including transposons, and potential new coding and noncoding genes. Our study resolves controversial areas of the <i>Oryza</i> phylogeny, showing a complex history of introgression among different chromosomes in the young ‘AA’ subclade containing the two domesticated species. This study highlights the prevalence of functionally coupled disease resistance genes and identifies many new haplotypes of potential use for future crop protection. Finally, this study marks a milestone in modern rice research with the release of a complete long-read assembly of IR 8 ‘Miracle Rice’, which relieved famine and drove the Green Revolution in Asia 50 years ago.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i> stands out in its significance to human civilization and food security with two species having been independently domesticated as rice: <i>Oryza sativa</i> in Asia ~10,000 years ago and <i>Oryza glaberrima</i> in Africa ~3,000 years ago. Today, Asian rice is the staple food for half the world, contributing an estimated 20% of human dietary calories (FAO Fact Sheet; see URLs). As the global population is projected to increase by almost 3 billion by 2050, rice breeders urgently need to develop new and sustainable cultivars with higher yields, healthier grains and reduced environmental footprints. Because the wild relatives of rice are adapted to different biogeographic ranges (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig1">1a</a>) and can tolerate many biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Atwell, B. J., Wang, H. &amp; Scafaro, A. P. Could abiotic stress tolerance in wild relatives of rice be used to improve Oryza sativa? Plant Sci. 215–216, 48–58 (2014)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1676">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Giuliani, R. et al. Coordination of leaf photosynthesis, transpiration, and structural traits in rice and wild relatives (Genus Oryza). Plant Physiol. 162, 1632–1651 (2013)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1676_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mizuta, Y., Harushima, Y. &amp; Kurata, N. Rice pollen hybrid incompatibility caused by reciprocal gene loss of duplicated genes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 20417–20422 (2010)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1676_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garg, R. et al. Deep transcriptome sequencing of wild halophyte rice, Porteresia coarctata, provides novel insights into the salinity and submergence tolerance factors. DNA Res. 21, 69–84 (2014)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1676_3">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iwamatsu, Y. et al. UVB sensitivity and cyclobutane pyrimidine dimer (CPD) photolyase genotypes in cultivated and wild rice species. Photochem. Photobiol. Sci. 7, 311–320 (2008)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1676_4">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="He, R. et al. A systems-wide comparison of red rice (Oryza longistaminata) tissues identifies rhizome specific genes and proteins that are targets for cultivated rice improvement. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 46 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1679">6</a></sup>, they constitute an important reservoir for crop improvement. Strategies to harness such traits for crop improvement show clear promise, as exemplified by the introgression of bacterial blight resistance (<i>Xa21</i>) from the wild species <i>Oryza longistaminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Song, W. Y. et al. A receptor kinase-like protein encoded by the rice disease resistance gene, Xa21. Science 270, 1804–1806 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1689">7</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Geographic distribution and phylogenetic reconstruction of wild species of Oryza and Leersia."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Geographic distribution and phylogenetic reconstruction of wild species of <i>Oryza</i> and <i>Leersia</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="616" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Geographic ranges of wild <i>Oryza</i> species and the outgroup species <i>L. perrieri</i> sequenced in this study. Dashed red lines show the limits of rice cultivation. Mapped ranges are adapted from IRRI’s Knowledge Bank (see URLs). <b>b</b>, Most frequently estimated species phylogeny in supermatrix and MP-EST analyses of each chromosome (inferred in 10 of 12 chromosomes and 11 of 12 chromosomes, respectively). Bootstrap proportions were obtained from 400 supermatrix bootstrap replicates, and MP-EST support values were obtained as described in the Methods. All clades were supported by both methods at 100%, except as indicated. Divergence times within the <i>Oryza</i> genus were estimated by PATHd8, assuming an age of 15 million years for the <i>Oryza</i> crown group. Bars on nodes represent the range of PATHd8 age estimates across all 12 chromosomes. Mya, million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The 27 <i>Oryza</i> species span ~15 million years of evolution, with 11 genome types, 6 of which are diploid (<i>n</i> = 12: AA, BB, CC, EE, FF and GG) and 5 of which are polyploid (<i>n</i> = 24: BBCC, CCDD, HHJJ, HHKK and KKLL). Cultivated rice belongs to the AA genome group, the primary germplasm pool for rice improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Jena, K. K. The species of the genus Oryza and transfer of useful genes from wild species into cultivated rice, O. sativa. Breed. Sci. 60, 518–523 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1749">8</a></sup>. To harness more distantly related species as genetic resources, a better understanding of genomic differences and similarities is essential.</p><p>As a first step toward a deeper evolutionary understanding of the genus, we developed a large array of publicly available genomic tools from BAC libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1756">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. The Oryza BAC resource: a genus-wide and genome scale tool for exploring rice genome evolution and leveraging useful genetic diversity from wild relatives. Breed. Sci. 60, 536–543 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1759">10</a></sup> and BAC-end sequences to physical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1763">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kim, H. et al. Comparative physical mapping between Oryza sativa (AA genome type) and O. punctata (BB genome type). Genetics 176, 379–390 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1766">12</a></sup> and subgenome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Rounsley, S. et al. De novo next generation sequencing of plant genomes. Rice 2, 35–43 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1770">13</a></sup>. Comparisons of select orthologous regions have addressed the origin and evolutionary fate of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Long-range and targeted ectopic recombination between the two homeologous chromosomes 11 and 12 in Oryza species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 3139–3150 (2011)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1774">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jacquemin, J., Laudié, M. &amp; Cooke, R. A recent duplication revisited: phylogenetic analysis reveals an ancestral duplication highly-conserved throughout the Oryza genus and beyond. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 146 (2009)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1774_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zhao, Y. et al. Identification and analysis of unitary loss of long-established protein-coding genes in Poaceae shows evidences for biased gene loss and putatively functional transcription of relics. BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 66 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1777">16</a></sup>, as well as transposable element (TE) dynamics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of Oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1781">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Fifteen million years of evolution in the Oryza genus shows extensive gene family expansion. Mol. Plant 7, 642–656 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1781_1">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sanyal, A. et al. Orthologous comparisons of the Hd1 region across genera reveal Hd1 gene lability within diploid Oryza species and disruptions to microsynteny in Sorghum. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 2487–2506 (2010)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1781_2">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1781_3">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Evolutionary dynamics of an ancient retrotransposon family provides insights into evolution of genome size in the genus Oryza. Plant J. 52, 342–351 (2007)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1781_4">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1784">22</a></sup> and the effects of polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1789">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Spatio-temporal patterns of genome evolution in allotetraploid species of the genus Oryza. Plant J. 63, 430–442 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1792">23</a></sup>. In addition, assemblies of several AA genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1796">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Genome and comparative transcriptomics of African wild rice Oryza longistaminata provide insights into molecular mechanism of rhizomatousness and self-incompatibility. Mol. Plant 8, 1683–1686 (2015)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1796_1">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1796_2">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, J. et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 113, E5163–E5171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1799">27</a></sup> and the <i>O. brachyantha</i> FF genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chen, J. F. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1806">28</a></sup> have already pointed to the power of complete genome comparisons.</p><p>Here we detail the generation and analysis of nine new reference assemblies that span cultivated and wild species in the <i>Oryza</i> genus and extend to the African outgroup species <i>Leersia perrieri</i>. Our comparative evolutionary genomics analysis includes these and four previously published genomes (<i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> (AA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1825">29</a></sup> and <i>indica</i> (AA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1833">30</a></sup>, <i>O. glaberrima</i> (AA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Genome and comparative transcriptomics of African wild rice Oryza longistaminata provide insights into molecular mechanism of rhizomatousness and self-incompatibility. Mol. Plant 8, 1683–1686 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1840">25</a></sup> and O. <i>brachyantha</i> (FF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chen, J. F. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1847">28</a></sup>), which were together reannotated using a common pipeline with the integration of baseline RNA-seq and bisulfite sequencing data.</p><p>Our analysis of this 13-genome dataset revealed several salient features of genome evolution in <i>Oryza</i>:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">1.</span>
                    
                      <p>A single-species phylogeny, supported by over 6,000 single-copy orthologs, places the ‘crown’ age of the AA clade at ~2.5 million years, implying a diversification rate (~0.50 net new species/million years) that is comparable to the high rates estimated for diverse plants in a variety of continental and island biodiversity hotspots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Madriñán, S., Cortés, A. J. &amp; Richardson, J. E. Páramo is the world’s fastest evolving and coolest biodiversity hotspot. Front. Genet. 4, 192 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4418e1868">31</a></sup>;</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">2.</span>
                    
                      <p>Extensive interspecific gene flow, particularly between the South American <i>Oryza glumaepatula</i> and African AA species, underscores the semipermeable nature of <i>Oryza</i> species boundaries, at least within AA-genome species;</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">3.</span>
                    
                      <p>The emergence of many putative taxonomically restricted gene families corresponds to higher lability, reduced coding length and higher rates of evolution than in ancient families. Along with long intergenic noncoding RNA (lincRNA) genes, members of new families are more likely to localize to repetitive regions of the genome, suggesting a possible role of TEs and reduced meiotic recombination in their origin;</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">4.</span>
                    
                      <p>Rapid diversification and turnover of LTR-retrotransposons largely differentiate the AA, BB, FF and <i>Leersia</i> genome types;</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">5.</span>
                    
                      <p>A significant preference of deletion over insertion mutations was detected across eight AA <i>Oryza</i> genomes, similar to in metazoan lineages;</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">6.</span>
                    
                      <p>Analysis of thousands of candidate disease resistance genes shows an overabundance of heterogeneous gene pairs organized in head-to-head fashion and with atypical domains, extending support for the integrated decoy model of disease resistance function.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                </ol></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">The 13-genome data package</h3><p>Chromosome-level reference assemblies for two domesticated (<i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> cv. IR 8 (also known as Miracle Rice) and the drought-tolerant <i>aus</i> variety N 22) and seven wild species (<i>Oryza rufipogon</i>, <i>Oryza nivara</i>, <i>Oryza barthii</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>Oryza meridionalis</i>, <i>Oryza punctata</i> and <i>L. perrieri</i>) were generated using either long-read or short-read technologies, with extensive scaffold support from long-insert library reads, including BAC-ends, as summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Tab1">1</a> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Extensive assessments of the quality of each assembly (for example, sequence accuracy, genome completeness and gene-space coverage) are described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">22</a>) and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM3">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM4">2</a>. These assemblies, plus the four additional published assemblies described above, were annotated using a uniform annotation pipeline to minimize methodological artifacts (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">14</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">25</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation statistics of 13 Oryzeae reference genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Species tree</h3><p>The outline of the <i>Oryza</i> phylogeny is clear, but the exact relationship between AA genomes, including the two domesticated rice species, has remained elusive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cranston, K. et al. Phylogenomic analysis of BAC-end sequence libraries in Oryza (Poaceae). Syst. Bot. 35, 512–523 (2010)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2629">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhu, T. et al. Phylogenetic relationships and genome divergence among the AA-genome species of the genus Oryza as revealed by 53 nuclear genes and 16 intergenic regions. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 70, 348–361 (2014)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2629_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zwickl, D. J., Stein, J. C., Wing, R. A., Ware, D. &amp; Sanderson, M. J. Disentangling methodological and biological sources of gene tree discordance on Oryza (Poaceae) chromosome 3. Syst. Biol. 63, 645–659 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2632">34</a></sup> owing to extensive gene tree discordance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Degnan, J. H. &amp; Rosenberg, N. A. Gene tree discordance, phylogenetic inference and the multispecies coalescent. Trends Ecol. Evol. 24, 332–340 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2636">35</a></sup>. Previous work has rarely used ‘species tree’ inference methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Degnan, J. H. &amp; Rosenberg, N. A. Gene tree discordance, phylogenetic inference and the multispecies coalescent. Trends Ecol. Evol. 24, 332–340 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2640">35</a></sup> that explicitly incorporate gene tree discordance caused by factors such as incomplete lineage sorting, and, if it did, did so with only a few genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Molina, J. et al. Molecular evidence for a single evolutionary origin of domesticated rice. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 8351–8356 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2644">36</a></sup>. Using 6,015 single-copy orthologs from ten <i>Oryza</i> genomes including <i>L. perrieri</i>, we inferred strong support for a single-species phylogeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">26</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), which supports two independent origins of <i>japonica</i> and <i>indica</i> rice. We note, however, that some gene tree conflicts cannot be explained by incomplete lineage sorting alone. For example, data for chromosomes 6 and 12 suggested that the South American AA species <i>O. glumaepatula</i> is the sister group only of African <i>O. barthii</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i>, rather than of these two species plus the <i>O. sativa</i> complex, as inferred from the whole-genome tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>With an inferred crown age of the AA species of less than 2.5 million years (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">27</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), footprints of past gene flow among AA species might still be detectible. Regions with evidence of introgression based on a positive <i>D</i> statistic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Durand, E. Y., Patterson, N., Reich, D. &amp; Slatkin, M. Testing for ancient admixture between closely related populations. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2239–2252 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2712">37</a></sup> were found on all chromosomes, with ten having significant introgressions between <i>O. glumaepatula</i> and the African but not the Asian species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">28</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="ABBA-BABA analysis of introgression in the AA-genome species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: ABBA-BABA analysis of introgression in the AA-genome species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="736" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Plot of <i>z</i> scores for the <i>D</i> statistic for each chromosome. Values above 3.0 are highly significant. Each <i>z</i> score is signed, with positive values indicating that the introgression involved the pair of species on the species tree in <b>b</b> indicated by the dashed arrow. <b>b</b>, Subtree restricted to four species from the AA-genome clade.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Another source of topological incongruence is gene conversion between duplicated regions retained after the pan-grass polyploidy event. Such regions are particularly common on the distal regions of chromosomes 11 and 12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Long-range and targeted ectopic recombination between the two homeologous chromosomes 11 and 12 in Oryza species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 3139–3150 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2765">14</a></sup>. Recent (&lt;4.9 million years in age) large-scale conversion events were apparent on the distal 2.2-Mb regions of both of these chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">6</a>). From inter- and intraspecies comparisons, we inferred four independent conversion events, one in the <i>L. perrieri</i> lineage and one each in the AA, BB and FF lineages (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">29</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">30</a>; also see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), with no further events since the divergence of the AA species. Dates calculated for <i>O. meridionalis</i>, the sister group to the other AA species analyzed, suggest that this common event occurred almost concomitantly with the divergence of the AA clade.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Karyotype conservation</h3><p>The 12-chromosome karyotype in rice resulted from the pan-grass polyploidy event and has deviated little in rice in comparison to other extant grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, X. et al. Genome alignment spanning major Poaceae lineages reveals heterogeneous evolutionary rates and alters inferred dates for key evolutionary events. Mol. Plant 8, 885–898 (2015)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2796">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 20, 1545–1557 (2010)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2796_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Murat, F. et al. Shared subgenome dominance following polyploidization explains grass genome evolutionary plasticity from a seven protochromosome ancestor with 16K protogenes. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 12–33 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2799">40</a></sup>. Using synteny maps and whole-genome alignments, we found that the outgroup species <i>L. perrieri</i> has many small inversions of five or more genes when compared to <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2808">29</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">7</a>). To estimate the rate of such events within <i>Oryza</i>, we focused on inversions shared by at least two consecutive species in the species tree, while discounting species-specific events as possible assembly errors. With <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> as an additional outgroup, we identified nine paracentric inversions, ranging from 60 to 300 kb in length and involving up to 19 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">31</a>), from which we estimate that such inversions arise about once every 1.6 million years. However, this rate is not constant, as there was only one event between the divergence of FF genomes and the AA–BB ancestor. In contrast, there were at least six inversions in the branch leading to the common ancestor of the AA-type genomes, more than double the average rate.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Chromosomal inversion events within the Oryza lineage."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Chromosomal inversion events within the <i>Oryza</i> lineage.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="423" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, An AA-genome-specific inversion (~170 kb, bearing 14 orthologous genes) took place approximately 4.4 million years after the split with BB-genome species (cladogram branch lengths not to scale). <b>b</b>, Placement of nine shared inversion events in the <i>Oryza</i> species tree. Inversions were detected as syntenic blocks of five or more genes in reverse orientation as compared to the <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> reference and were verified using the <i>B. distachyon</i> outgroup as representing the ancestral state. Nodes are labeled with letters as in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">4</a>. The length of the branches (units million years; not drawn to scale) is indicated beneath the branches, while the number of scored inversion events is indicated above the branches. The approximate rate of inversion is shown in red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">TE diversification and rapid elimination</h3><p>Selective amplification and loss of TEs has had a key role in <i>Oryza</i> genome and chromosome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of Oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2884">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2887">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Evolutionary dynamics of an ancient retrotransposon family provides insights into evolution of genome size in the genus Oryza. Plant J. 52, 342–351 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2890">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Uozu, S. et al. Repetitive sequences: cause for variation in genome size and chromosome morphology in the genus Oryza. Plant Mol. Biol. 35, 791–799 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2893">41</a></sup>. Repeats, including TEs, constitute 27–50% of the genome assemblies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">20</a>), providing a unique basis for a comprehensive genus-wide comparison of TE dynamics. Each species was distinguished by its diversity of long terminal repeat–retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), the most abundant TE class<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2903">26</a></sup>. Comparative annotation and phylogenetic analysis of LTR-RTs identified several lineage-specific transpositional bursts (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">9</a>, and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">32</a>), most within the last 2.5 million years (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig4">4</a>). Because of their young average age, the LTR-RT populations of the different species are distinct. For example, six families that amplified specifically in the BB species <i>O. punctata</i> (families 11, 26, 27, 36, 48 and 50; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">32</a>) occupy almost 25 Mb (or 6.3% of the assembly). Even among the closely related Asian AA species, there are marked differences in RETRO 1/2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2927">42</a></sup> and Spip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vitte, C., Chaparro, C., Quesneville, H. &amp; Panaud, O. Spip and Squiq, two novel rice non-autonomous LTR retro-element families related to RIRE3 and RIRE8. Plant Sci. 172, 8–19 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2931">43</a></sup> complements (family 7; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">10</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">32</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Abundance and age distribution of LTR-RT insertions belonging to the Copia and Gypsy superfamilies and unclassified elements in 11 Oryzeae genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Abundance and age distribution of LTR-RT insertions belonging to the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> superfamilies and unclassified elements in 11 Oryzeae genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1560" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Box-and-whisker plots show the median and upper and lower quartiles of insertion age, while the width of each ‘violin’ is proportional to element abundance (plots not scaled between superfamilies).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Rapid loss of LTR-RTs can greatly reduce TE diversity and limit genome size increases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR–retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860–869 (2004)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2970">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2970_1">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Vitte, C., Panaud, O. &amp; Quesneville, H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics 8, 218 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2973">46</a></sup>. Tracking 75 orthologous LTR-RT loci across the eight most closely related AA species (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM5">3</a>) yielded an average loss rate of 3.62 kb/million years per element. Applying this ratio to the whole genome predicts that approximately one-quarter of the <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> genome will be eliminated within the next 3–4 million years unless new waves of transposition occur during the same period. Similar to previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR–retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860–869 (2004)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2986">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2986_1">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Vitte, C., Panaud, O. &amp; Quesneville, H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics 8, 218 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4418e2989">46</a></sup>, our analysis extends these findings across the AA genomes of the <i>Oryza</i> genus.</p><p>These results reinforce the notion that TEs are important drivers of genome evolution because they fuel the rapid turnover of intergenic regions. However, not all TEs behave equally: the terminal-repeat retrotransposons in miniature (TRIMs), which are mostly located near genes, were highly conserved among all 13 genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">33</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Indels as drivers of genome evolution and domestication</h3><p>Pairwise comparisons of the eight most closely related AA genomes identified 216,059–699,587 indels, depending on the genome comparison, with the ancestral state determined for about half of these (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">34</a>). In agreement with previous work in <i>Oryza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3020">26</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Schneeberger, K. et al. Reference-guided assembly of four diverse Arabidopsis thaliana genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 10249–10254 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3026">47</a></sup>, short indels were strongly favored. In coding regions, in-frame indels at multiples of 3 bp were over-represented, as expected (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">12</a>). There was a significant bias toward deletions (chi-squared test, <i>P</i> &lt; 8.96 × 10<sup>–11</sup>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">35</a>), similar to findings in metazoan lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Gregory, T. R. Insertion–deletion biases and the evolution of genome size. Gene 324, 15–34 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3042">48</a></sup>.</p><p>To better understand indel population dynamics and the role of domestication, we expanded this study to include 20 accessions of cultivated <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 19 of its wild progenitor, <i>O. barthii</i>. From medium-depth resequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3055">24</a></sup>, we identified 162,267 additional indels in <i>O. glaberrima</i> and 448,000 in <i>O. barthii</i>. Pairwise diversity <i>π</i> was greater in <i>O. barthii</i> (0.622/kb) than in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (0.446/kb), consistent with a genetic bottleneck and strong selection during <i>O. glaberrima</i> domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3078">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Meyer, R. S. et al. Domestication history and geographical adaptation inferred from a SNP map of African rice. Nat. Genet. 48, 1083–1088 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3081">49</a></sup>. To further test this hypothesis, we plotted the derived site-frequency spectrum across 80,507 and 41,947 indels (&gt;1 bp) within <i>O. barthii</i> or <i>O. glaberrima</i>, with the ancestral state inferred from <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> and <i>O. glumaepatula</i> as outgroups. A U-shaped frequency distribution was observed only in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">13</a>), indicating a much higher fixation rate of polymorphic indels in the domesticated species than in its progenitor. <i>O. glaberrima</i> was previously shown to have been derived from subpopulations of <i>O. barthii</i> more than 3,000 years ago, with an attendant population bottleneck during early cultivation and domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Meyer, R. S. et al. Domestication history and geographical adaptation inferred from a SNP map of African rice. Nat. Genet. 48, 1083–1088 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3114">49</a></sup>. Thus, demographic history shaped by artificial selection could largely explain the high frequency of fixed mutations (984 insertions and 2,110 deletions) in <i>O. glaberrima</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">35</a>). Indels shared by &gt;90% of individuals were enriched in 72 putative domestication loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3124">24</a></sup> as compared to genome background (5.2 versus 3.1 per 100 kb; <i>t</i> test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01), pointing to indels as being particularly important during domestication. These observations broadly resemble observations with SNP data from Asian cultivated rice and its progenitor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Caicedo, A. L. et al. Genome-wide patterns of nucleotide polymorphism in domesticated rice. PLoS Genet. 3, 1745–1756 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3135">50</a></sup>.</p><p>To understand the evolutionary rates of indel formation in the non-cultivated species, we note that the single-genome comparisons (<i>O. barthii</i> versus <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> versus <i>O. glaberrima</i>) identified up to 170,903 and 46,846 indels in <i>O. barthii</i>, but none had become fixed since <i>O. barthii</i> split from <i>O. glaberrima</i>. This observation suggests that most indels inferred from comparisons of just single genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">34</a>) are still polymorphic within populations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Oryzeae- and species-specific gene families</h3><p>We identified 21,448 putative gene families, which were assigned to four age bins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">14</a>). The most ancient group, dated to at least the common ancestor of angiosperms, included 62% of genes but only 29% of families. The Poaceae age group, comprising a further 13% of genes and 9% of families, was enriched in defense response (GO:0006952) and wound response (GO:0009611) genes, including those encoding disease resistance receptors (see below) and enzyme inhibitors (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">36</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM6">4</a>). In addition, these families were enriched in genes encoding components of the ubiquitin–proteasome pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Choi, C. M., Gray, W. M., Mooney, S. &amp; Hellmann, H. Composition, roles, and regulation of cullin-based ubiquitin E3 ligases. Arabidopsis Book 12, e0175 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3190">51</a></sup>, including SKP1/BTB/POZ and F-box proteins, the latter of which are known to undergo lineage-specific expansions and rapid evolution in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Navarro-Quezada, A., Schumann, N. &amp; Quint, M. Plant F-box protein evolution is determined by lineage-specific timing of major gene family expansion waves. PLoS One 8, e68672 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3195">52</a></sup>. Also enriched in Poaceae were genes encoding two classes of proteins abundant in late embryogenesis, which function in dehydration and other stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Hincha, D. K. &amp; Thalhammer, A. LEA proteins: IDPs with versatile functions in cellular dehydration tolerance. Biochem. Soc. Trans. 40, 1000–1003 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3199">53</a></sup>. Genes that emerged within the Oryzeae accounted for 60% of families and 19% of annotated loci and were enriched in seed storage proteins, defense-related genes and nucleic-acid-binding functions (GO:0003676), including various DNA-binding domains, histones and methyl-CpG DNA-binding proteins (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">37</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM6">4</a>). Species-specific families and orphans accounted for an average of 6% of annotated loci per species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Evidence supporting a protogene model of putative new gene origination in the Oryzeae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Evidence supporting a protogene model of putative new gene origination in the Oryzeae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Assignment of annotated loci in <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> to age ranks by inference of most recent common ancestor based on Compara (Methods) gene families. <b>b</b>, Coding length and transcript expression are positively correlated with age (<i>τ</i> and <i>p</i>, Kendall’s correlation statistics). Box plots show distributions of mean coding length per family or the expression value of the locus with the highest expression per family, with trendlines connecting median values (<i>n</i> = 2,144, 409, 913, 1,222, 2,176, 1,551, 794, 223, 676, 171, 1,440 and 5,975 families assigned to age ranks 0 through 11, respectively. <b>c</b>, <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ratio and <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> substitution rates are negatively correlated with age (<i>τ</i> and <i>p</i>, Kendall’s correlation statistics). Values were calculated relative to syntenic orthologs in <i>O. nivara</i>. Box plots show distributions of the lowest value per family, with trendlines connecting medians (<i>n</i> = 506, 556, 1,272, 923, 563, 153, 579, 142, 1,328 and 5,768 families assigned to age ranks 2 through 11, respectively).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>While these analyses show that lineage-specific gene families can encode proteins with recognized biological functions, only 6% of species-specific and 10% of Oryzeae-specific loci have predicted InterPro domains, as compared to 63% in Poaceae and 91% in angiosperm-derived families. Despite some methodological concerns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bennetzen, J. L., Coleman, C., Liu, R., Ma, J. &amp; Ramakrishna, W. Consistent over-estimation of gene number in complex plant genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 7, 732–736 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3286">54</a></sup>, it is widely recognized that new genes arise continuously across all domains of life<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Long, M., VanKuren, N. W., Chen, S. &amp; Vibranovski, M. D. New gene evolution: little did we know. Annu. Rev. Genet. 47, 307–333 (2013)." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3290">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Arendsee, Z. W., Li, L. &amp; Wurtele, E. S. Coming of age: orphan genes in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 19, 698–708 (2014)." href="#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3290_1">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Schlötterer, C. Genes from scratch—the evolutionary fate of de novo genes. Trends Genet. 31, 215–219 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3293">57</a></sup>. The fraction of Oryzeae-specific genes with syntenic orthologs in a second species was not substantially lower than for the two older groups, with 86% as compared to 93% and 97% for the Poaceae and angiosperm-derived families, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">38</a>). This in turn provides unique opportunities to study patterns and mechanisms of putative new gene origination within a phylogenetic framework.</p><p>New functional genes likely evolve via transitory protogenes; while most are rapidly lost, a subset will be positively selected and gradually gain the characteristics of deeply rooted genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Carvunis, A. R. et al. Proto-genes and de novo gene birth. Nature 487, 370–374 (2012)." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3303">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Neme, R. &amp; Tautz, D. Phylogenetic patterns of emergence of new genes support a model of frequent de novo evolution. BMC Genomics 14, 117 (2013)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3303_1">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Palmieri, N., Kosiol, C. &amp; Schlötterer, C. The life cycle of Drosophila orphan genes. eLife 3, e01311 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3306">60</a></sup>. A key prediction is that properties such as coding length, expression, substitution rate and evolutionary stability change in an age-dependent manner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Carvunis, A. R. et al. Proto-genes and de novo gene birth. Nature 487, 370–374 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3310">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Wolf, Y. I., Novichkov, P. S., Karev, G. P., Koonin, E. V. &amp; Lipman, D. J. The universal distribution of evolutionary rates of genes and distinct characteristics of eukaryotic genes of different apparent ages. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 7273–7280 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3313">61</a></sup>.</p><p>Coding length showed clear age dependence, with the median length increasing several fold from the youngest to the oldest group (Wilcoxon rank-sum test, <i>P</i> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">15</a>). This held true even when excluding genes shared throughout the angiosperms (Kendall’s tau, <i>P</i> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig5">5b</a>). This observed age dependence, also found in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Arendsee, Z. W., Li, L. &amp; Wurtele, E. S. Coming of age: orphan genes in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 19, 698–708 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3339">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Guo, Y. L. Gene family evolution in green plants with emphasis on the origination and evolution of Arabidopsis thaliana genes. Plant J. 73, 941–951 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3342">62</a></sup>, may indicate that longer initial coding lengths are more likely to survive over time or that coding sequences that survive gradually lengthen over time through chance removal of stop codons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Carvunis, A. R. et al. Proto-genes and de novo gene birth. Nature 487, 370–374 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3346">58</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene expression was also positively correlated with age, both when assessed qualitatively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">14</a>) and quantitatively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig5">5b</a>). Again, the exclusion of angiosperm-derived genes from the analysis did not change this finding (Kendall’s tau, <i>P</i> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig5">5b</a>). These findings are consistent with lower and more restricted expression of lineage-specific loci in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Donoghue, M. T., Keshavaiah, C., Swamidatta, S. H. &amp; Spillane, C. Evolutionary origins of Brassicaceae specific genes in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 47 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3369">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wu, D.-D. et al. “Out of pollen” hypothesis for origin of new genes in flowing plants: study from Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 2822–2829 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3372">64</a></sup>.</p><p>Relaxed negative selection is a common characteristic of lineage-specific genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Schlötterer, C. Genes from scratch—the evolutionary fate of de novo genes. Trends Genet. 31, 215–219 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3379">57</a></sup>. Comparisons of syntenic orthologs confirmed a trend of relaxed selection in younger genes (for example, median <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> for Oryzeae (0.59) &gt; Poaceae (0.42) &gt; angiosperms (0.27)), concomitant with higher rates for both nonsynonymous and synonymous substitutions (Wilcoxon rank-sum test, <i>P</i> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">16</a>). Nevertheless, the median <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value for Oryzeae-specific genes was well below neutral rates, suggesting that many encode functional proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Prabh, N. &amp; Rödelsperger, C. Are orphan genes protein-coding, prediction artifacts, or non-coding RNAs? BMC Bioinformatics 17, 226 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3408">65</a></sup>.
