These observed trends also held true after fractionating over all nodes in the species tree. Using measurements for <i>O. nivara</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig5">5c</a>), we found a significant negative correlation for <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> with age, even when restricting to nodes within the Oryzeae (Kendall’s tau, <i>P</i> &lt; 1.7 × 10<sup>−6</sup>).</p><p>A final expectation is greater evolutionary stability of older genes. On average, any one species had 96% of families shared through the angiosperms but only 76% of Poaceae-specific and 15% of Oryzeae-specific families. Similarly, 75% of families shared through the angiosperms were present in all species but only 34% of Poaceae-specific and 15% of Oryzeae-specific families were (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">14</a>). The oldest families were also the largest, consistent with expectations that fixed families diversify over time. Finally, Oryzeae-specific loci were more likely to be found near centromeres over low-recombination regions, and thus more likely to reside closer to LRT-RTs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig6">6</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Positional bias of loci derived from ancient and recent families in the Oryzeae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Positional bias of loci derived from ancient and recent families in the Oryzeae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="330" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Each segment represents a stacked histogram of tiled windows of 100 loci. Species: 1, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i>; 2, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>aus</i> (N 22); 3, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (IR 8); 4, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (93-11); 5, <i>O. rufipogon</i>; 6, <i>O. nivara</i>; 7, <i>O. glaberrima</i>; 8, <i>O. barthii</i>; 9, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>; 10, <i>O. meridionalis</i>; 11, <i>O. punctata</i>; 12, <i>O. brachyantha</i>; 13, <i>L. perrieri</i>. Recombination rate is shown in the outer ring, ranging from 0 to 9 cM/Mb, based on the integrated genetic/physical map of rice (constructed from data from Harushima et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Harushima, Y. et al. A high-density rice genetic linkage map with 2275 markers using a single F2 population. Genetics 148, 479–494 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3521">103</a></sup> and McCouch et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="McCouch, S. R. et al. Development and mapping of 2240 new SSR markers for rice (Oryza sativa L.). DNA Res. 9, 199–207 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3525">104</a></sup>) for <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> downloaded from http://archive.gramene.org/. Triangles show the position of rice centromeres (see URLs). <b>b</b>, Differential correlations in <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> of gene age group prevalence (calculated over 191 2-Mb non-overlapping windows) with chromosome recombination rate. Plots show individual points color-coded by chromosome fitted by simple linear regression. Pearson’s correlation coefficient (<i>r</i>) and <i>P</i> value are shown for each plot.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Chimeric genes are common in <i>O. sativa</i>, suggesting that new genes originate at a much higher frequency than in animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wang, W. et al. High rate of chimeric gene origination by retroposition in plant genomes. Plant Cell 18, 1791–1802 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3565">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Zhang, C. et al. High occurrence of functional new chimeric genes in survey of rice chromosome 3 short arm genome sequences. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 1038–1048 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3568">67</a></sup>. One mechanism of chimeric gene formation is the transposition of genic sequences captured by flanking MULE DNA transposons in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Jiang, N., Bao, Z., Zhang, X., Eddy, S. R. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Pack-MULE transposable elements mediate gene evolution in plants. Nature 431, 569–573 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3572">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Hanada, K. et al. The functional role of pack-MULEs in rice inferred from purifying selection and expression profile. Plant Cell 21, 25–38 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3575">69</a></sup>. MULE-derived loci (MDLs) accounted for 7% of Oryzeae- and species-specific genes. While this is a small minority, it is clearly much higher than for all genes (chi-squared test, <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−32</sup>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">39</a>). MDLs in AA genomes showed high rates of microsynteny (68–92%), indicative of conserved genomic position over periods of up to 2.4 million years. MDLs showed different characteristics from non-gene-containing MULEs, having for example higher GC content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Ferguson, A. A., Zhao, D. &amp; Jiang, N. Selective acquisition and retention of genomic sequences by Pack-Mutator-like elements based on guanine–cytosine content and the breadth of expression. Plant Physiol. 163, 1419–1432 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3588">70</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">18</a>). Methylation levels of MDLs were also lower and changed with evolutionary age<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Wang, J. et al. DNA methylation changes facilitated evolution of genes derived from Mutator-like transposable elements. Genome Biol. 17, 92 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3595">71</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">LincRNA genes as a source of genetic novelty</h3><p>We found thousands of lincRNAs that did not overlap protein-coding genes. <i>L. perrieri</i> had more lincRNAs than any <i>Oryza</i> species (<i>q</i> ≤ 3 × 10<sup>–31</sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">20</a>), while the two domesticated species, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i>, had significantly fewer than their wild ancestors (<i>O. glaberrima</i> versus <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>q </i>≤ 5 × 10<sup>–20</sup>; <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> versus <i>O. rufipogon</i>, <i>q </i>≤ 4 × 10<sup>−73</sup>). These differences cannot be explained by different RNA-seq coverage (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">21</a>). Most lincRNA families have evolved very recently, with the majority being species specific (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">22</a>). Of the 23,633 families identified in nine species, over 91% were species specific and only 101 lincRNA families (&lt;0.5%) were detectable as far back as the common ancestor of <i>Oryza</i> and <i>L. perrieri</i>, indicating rapid emergence and turnover.</p><p>Similarly to vertebrates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kapusta, A. et al. Transposable elements are major contributors to the origin, diversification, and regulation of vertebrate long noncoding RNAs. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003470 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3673">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kelley, D. &amp; Rinn, J. Transposable elements reveal a stem cell–specific class of long noncoding RNAs. Genome Biol. 13, R107 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3676">73</a></sup>, lincRNAs were enriched with TE-derived sequences, especially ones from DNA transposons and LTR-RTs (found in &gt; 59%; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">23</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM7">5</a>). Whether this tolerance for TE sequence derives from non-functionality of the lincRNA transcripts or a modular structure that allows for large indels remains to be explored.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Evolution of disease resistance genes</h3><p>Most plant disease resistance genes encode intracellular nucleotide-binding, leucine-rich repeat (NLR) receptors that directly or indirectly recognize pathogen effector proteins. NLR content is highly variable among rice populations, accounting for the majority of copy number and presence/absence polymorphisms affecting genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schatz, M. C. et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa, document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 15, 506 (2014)." href="#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3694">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yu, P. et al. Genome-wide copy number variations in Oryza sativa L. BMC Genomics 14, 649 (2013)." href="#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3694_1">75</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Yao, W. et al. Exploring the rice dispensable genome using a metagenome-like assembly strategy. Genome Biol. 16, 187 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3697">76</a></sup>, explained by rapid evolution and balancing selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Leister, D. Tandem and segmental gene duplication and recombination in the evolution of plant disease resistance gene. Trends Genet. 20, 116–122 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3701">77</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Okuyama, Y. et al. A multifaceted genomics approach allows the isolation of the rice Pia-blast resistance gene consisting of two adjacent NBS-LRR protein genes. Plant J. 66, 467–479 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3704">78</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified 5,408 candidate NLR genes, ranging from 237 in <i>O. brachyantha</i> to 535 in <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (93–11) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">40</a>), distributed across 28 subfamilies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig7">7a</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">41</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">42</a>). Summation of duplication histories in gene trees showed that a previously reported expansion of NLR genes in the AA genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3733">26</a></sup> can be more precisely placed at the common ancestor of Asian and African rice, after the split of <i>O. meridionalis</i> and <i>O. glumaepatula</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">24</a>). In addition, NLR expansion was markedly higher in both the <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i> varietal groups as compared to their wild progenitors, consistent with recent artificial selection for increased NLR diversity.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Diversity of NLR genes in the Oryzeae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Diversity of NLR genes in the Oryzeae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0040-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_40_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="751" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Maximum-likelihood tree of the NB-ARC domains of 1,000 NLR-type disease resistance genes in three species: <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (93-11) (green), <i>O. punctata</i> (purple) and <i>L. perrieri</i> (orange). Designated gene families are lettered. Sixteen characterized NLR genes are indicated, as cited in the Methods. <b>b</b>, The rice <i>Pik1</i>–<i>Pik2</i> locus and encompassing heterogeneous NLR cluster showing a pattern of underlying conservation, including a prevalence of putatively coupled gene pairs (W:D pairs in head-to-head configuration, akin to <i>RGA4</i>–<i>RGA5</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Hayashi, K. &amp; Yoshida, H. Refunctionalization of the ancient rice blast disease resistance gene Pit by the recruitment of a retrotransposon as a promoter. Plant J. 57, 413–425 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3794">92</a></sup>. Non-canonical C-terminal domains, such as WRKY, which function as integrated decoys that recognize pathogen avirulence gene products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92–100 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3798">102</a></sup>, are found in 17 ‘<i>D</i>’ genes as labeled. The region shown corresponds to 11:27,594,830–27,990,888 on the <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare RefSeq sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3812">29</a></sup>. Osj Nip, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i>; Osa N22, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> vg. <i>aus</i> (N 22); Osi IR8, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (IR 8); Osi 9311, <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (93-11); Orufi, <i>O. rufipogon</i>; Oniva, <i>O. nivara</i>; Oglab, <i>O. glaberrima</i>; Obart, <i>O. barthii</i>; Oglum, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>; Omeri, <i>O. meridionalis</i>; Opunc, <i>O. punctate</i>; Obrac, <i>O. brachyantha</i>; Lperr, <i>L. perrieri.</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>As previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3883">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Zhou, T. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS genes in japonica rice reveals significant expansion of divergent non-TIR NBS-LRR genes. Mol. Genet. Genomics 271, 402–415 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3886">79</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Yang, S. et al. Genome-wide investigation on the genetic variations of rice disease resistance genes. Plant Mol. Biol. 62, 181–193 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3889">80</a></sup>, most NLR genes are positionally clustered (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">40</a>), with the greatest density on chromosome 11, a finding now extended to the <i>Leersia</i> genus (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">43</a>). One-third of the 1,046 clusters across 13 genomes had mixed subfamily composition (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">40</a>) indicating that they did not arise simply by local duplications of single progenitor genes. Recent studies have shown that disease resistance sometimes requires the joint action of two side-by-side NLR genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Okuyama, Y. et al. A multifaceted genomics approach allows the isolation of the rice Pia-blast resistance gene consisting of two adjacent NBS-LRR protein genes. Plant J. 66, 467–479 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3906">78</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cesari, S. et al. The rice resistance protein pair RGA4/RGA5 recognizes the Magnaporthe oryzae effectors AVR-Pia and AVR1-CO39 by direct binding. Plant Cell 25, 1463–1481 (2013)." href="#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3909">81</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, S. K. et al. Rice Pi5-mediated resistance to Magnaporthe oryzae requires the presence of two coiled-coil–nucleotide-binding–leucine-rich repeat genes. Genetics 181, 1627–1638 (2009)." href="#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3909_1">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Ashikawa, I. et al. Two adjacent nucleotide-binding site–leucine-rich repeat class genes are required to confer Pikm-specific rice blast resistance. Genetics 180, 2267–2276 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3912">83</a></sup>, suggesting that formation and maintenance of complex clusters may be driven by such functionally coupled pairs. As typified by rice <i>RGA4</i>–<i>RGA5</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Okuyama, Y. et al. A multifaceted genomics approach allows the isolation of the rice Pia-blast resistance gene consisting of two adjacent NBS-LRR protein genes. Plant J. 66, 467–479 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3921">78</a></sup> and <i>Pik-1</i>–<i>Pik-2</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Cesari, S., Bernoux, M., Moncuquet, P., Kroj, T. &amp; Dodds, P. N. A novel conserved mechanism for plant NLR protein pairs: the “integrated decoy” hypothesis. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 606 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3930">84</a></sup>, coupled NLRs are frequently distantly related, arranged in head-to-head formation (divergently transcribed) and include atypical domains that function as integrated decoy domains in pathogen recognition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Cesari, S., Bernoux, M., Moncuquet, P., Kroj, T. &amp; Dodds, P. N. A novel conserved mechanism for plant NLR protein pairs: the “integrated decoy” hypothesis. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 606 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3935">84</a></sup>.</p><p>To investigate the prevalence of these characteristics, we studied all 1,386 instances of adjacent NLR genes, of which over one-quarter were classified as heterogeneous (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">44</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">46</a>). Among these, the head-to-head configuration was significantly more prevalent than expected by chance (47% versus 25%; chi-squared test, <i>P</i> = 0.0001) and also more likely to show conserved synteny than other arrangements (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">25</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">45</a>). In contrast, homogeneous pairs showed significant bias for the head-to-tail arrangement (chi-squared test, <i>P</i> = 0.0001) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">45</a>), which may reflect underlying mechanisms of tandem duplication.</p><p>Across the phylogeny, we identified 230 NLR genes with unusual N-terminal domains and 239 NLR genes with unusual C-terminal domains, with 23 genes having both (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM7">5</a>). The most common domains match known integrated decoy domains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Sarris, P. F., Cevik, V., Dagdas, G., Jones, J. D. &amp; Krasileva, K. V. Comparative analysis of plant immune receptor architectures uncovers host proteins likely targeted by pathogens. BMC Biol. 14, 8 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3971">85</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Kroj, T., Chanclud, E., Michel-Romiti, C., Grand, X. &amp; Morel, J. B. Integration of decoy domains derived from protein targets of pathogen effectors into plant immune receptors is widespread. New Phytol. 210, 618–626 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d4418e3974">86</a></sup>, but many more rare types were also found. The presence of specific domains in different NLR subfamilies point to multiple, independent acquisitions of decoys by NLRs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">42</a>). Among adjacent NLR genes, putative integrated decoy domains were significantly more prevalent in heterogeneous pairs arranged in head-to-head configuration as compared to other classes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">45</a>; chi-squared test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). These trends with the <i>Pik-1</i>–<i>Pik-2</i> locus and adjacent cluster of <i>RGA4</i>–<i>RGA5</i>-like pairs are illustrated in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Fig7">7b</a>, with evidence for evolution of new specificities both within species and between species, by acquisition of WRKY, NAM, protein kinase, U-box and VQ domains. Finally, an analysis of the <i>Pi-ta</i> region (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">26</a>) identifies an excellent candidate for the tightly linked <i>R</i> gene <i>Pi-ta2</i>, whose broader resistance spectrum encompasses that of <i>Pi-ta</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Bryan, G. T. et al. A single amino acid difference distinguishes resistant and susceptible alleles of the rice blast resistance gene Pi-ta. Plant Cell 12, 2033–2046 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4018">87</a></sup> but which has not been positively identified because of very low recombination in the <i>Pi-ta</i> region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Bryan, G. T. et al. A single amino acid difference distinguishes resistant and susceptible alleles of the rice blast resistance gene Pi-ta. Plant Cell 12, 2033–2046 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4026">87</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>With two independently domesticated species, <i>Oryza</i> has had a significant role in advancing human civilization, leading some rice-dependent cultures to revere ‘rice is life’, as exemplified in dramatic fashion ~50 years ago with the release of IR8 in Asia. As the world population approaches 10 billion by 2050, rice breeders are faced with the challenge of producing crops that are high yielding and more nutritious while at the same time being more sustainable. How can we meet the challenge of producing more food and at the same time protecting the environment?</p><p>The premise of the International <i>Oryza</i> Map Alignment Project (IOMAP), initiated in 2003, was to develop a set of high-quality genomic resources for the wild relatives of rice that could be used as a foundational resource to discover and utilize novel genes, traits and/or genomic regions for crop improvement and basic research. IOMAP began with the generation of genomic tools (BAC libraries and physical maps) derived from wild accessions that have been actively used in breeding programs to introgress new traits into cultivated rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Jacquemin, J., Bhatia, D., Singh, K. &amp; Wing, R. A. The International Oryza Map Alignment Project: development of a genus-wide comparative genomics platform to help solve the 9 billion-people question. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 147–156 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4048">88</a></sup>. Only recently have viable F<sub>1</sub> hybrids been developed for all 25 wild species crossed with <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Jena, K. K. The species of the genus Oryza and transfer of useful genes from wild species into cultivated rice, O. sativa. Breed. Sci. 60, 518–523 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4056">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we describe the generation of new reference assemblies for six wild <i>Oryza</i> species (<i>O. nivara</i>, <i>O. rufipogon</i>, <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>O. meridionalis</i> and <i>O. punctata</i>), two domesticates (<i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> (IR 8) and <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>aus</i> (N 22)) and the closely related outgroup species <i>L. perrieri</i>. In combination with four previously published <i>Oryza</i> genomes, our dataset represents a genome-wide vista of the results of ~15 million years of both natural and artificial selection on a single genus. Over this time period, the Oryzeae have maintained a base chromosome number of 12, despite having adapted to different ecological conditions associated with their global distribution and withstanding bursts of TE diversification that, in some cases, led to doubling of genome sizes, as in <i>O. australiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4106">20</a></sup> and <i>O. granulata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Evolutionary dynamics of an ancient retrotransposon family provides insights into evolution of genome size in the genus Oryza. Plant J. 52, 342–351 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4112">21</a></sup>.</p><p>Our phylogenomic work illustrates both the challenges of inferring species phylogenies in closely related plant taxa—incomplete lineage sorting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Pease, J. B., Haak, D. C., Hahn, M. W. &amp; Moyle, L. C. Phylogenomics reveals three sources of adaptive variation during a rapid radiation. PLoS Biol. 14, e1002379 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4119">89</a></sup>, hybridization and introgression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Fontaine, M. C. et al. Extensive introgression in a malaria vector species complex revealed by phylogenomics. Science 347, 1258524 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4123">90</a></sup>—and the power of whole-genome sequences to untangle the resulting phylogenetic discordance. Combining recent tools for species tree inference with this massive dataset permitted us to construct a much more nuanced view of the species phylogeny in <i>Oryza</i> that reflects the mosaic history of different parts of the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Maddison, W. Gene trees in species trees. Syst. Biol. 46, 523–536 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4130">91</a></sup>. Not only does this allow a more complete framework for studies of the evolution of genes, chromosomes and genomes in <i>Oryza</i>, but it also leads to more accurate placement of <i>Oryza</i> species in an evolutionary and ecological context. For example, our genome-based estimate of the age of the AA genome clade implies a remarkably rapid diversification rate of ~0.50 net new species/million years, placing it on par with many rapidly diversifying taxa in island and continental hotspots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Madriñán, S., Cortés, A. J. &amp; Richardson, J. E. Páramo is the world’s fastest evolving and coolest biodiversity hotspot. Front. Genet. 4, 192 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4141">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhu, T. et al. Phylogenetic relationships and genome divergence among the AA-genome species of the genus Oryza as revealed by 53 nuclear genes and 16 intergenic regions. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 70, 348–361 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4144">33</a></sup>. This rapid species diversification among <i>Oryza</i> species in the AA clade likely provided special opportunities for cultivation and improvement by humans, resulting in two independent domestication events in this group.</p><p>Our analyses of TE-driven genome dynamics in <i>Oryza</i> point to a very high rate of TE turnover over long evolutionary periods. While this has been previously demonstrated in cultivated rice, it was unclear whether this pattern holds for the entire genus. We show that this process results from both recent lineage-specific TE amplifications and a high deletion rate of TE-related sequences. Even if <i>Oryza</i> genomes share many TE families, one could expect that the vast majority of TE insertions are polymorphic among wild rice species. Several recent studies have demonstrated the functional impact of TE-related sequences in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Hayashi, K. &amp; Yoshida, H. Refunctionalization of the ancient rice blast disease resistance gene Pit by the recruitment of a retrotransposon as a promoter. Plant J. 57, 413–425 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4161">92</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Zhang, H. et al. Transposon-derived small RNA is responsible for modified function of WRKY45 locus. Nat. Plants 2, 16016 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4164">93</a></sup> and other crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Butelli, E. et al. Retrotransposons control fruit-specific, cold-dependent accumulation of anthocyanins in blood oranges. Plant Cell 24, 1242–1255 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4168">94</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Ong-Abdullah, M. et al. Loss of Karma transposon methylation underlies the mantled somaclonal variant of oil palm. Nature 525, 533–537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4171">95</a></sup> on gene expression. TE polymorphisms in wild <i>Oryza</i> species could thus be exploited for breeding in addition to the diversity found in the genes themselves.</p><p>Comparative analysis of gene annotations across the Oryzeae revealed many apparent lineage-specific families that emerged in the common ancestor of grasses or within the Oryzeae clade. The latter exhibited characteristics typical of taxonomically restricted loci in plants and other domains of life, including shorter coding sequences, low expression, rapid evolution rates and family instability. Lack of recognizable functional domains also characterized these annotations, consistent with a de novo origin. Although such qualities can also be found in annotation artifacts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bennetzen, J. L., Coleman, C., Liu, R., Ma, J. &amp; Ramakrishna, W. Consistent over-estimation of gene number in complex plant genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 7, 732–736 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4181">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Prabh, N. &amp; Rödelsperger, C. Are orphan genes protein-coding, prediction artifacts, or non-coding RNAs? BMC Bioinformatics 17, 226 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4184">65</a></sup>, the Oryzeae-specific loci (as distinguished from species-specific loci) showed a surprising degree of conserved synteny, even among more distantly related species, and we therefore focused on this subset to examine the effects of age on these characteristics. Our finding of a clear correlation toward more ‘gene-like’ characteristics with increasing age is consistent with Oryzeae-specific loci representing a pool of rapidly evolving sequences with adaptive potential. The observed continuum of characteristics over evolutionary time extended to the older, more established gene families, with characteristics of Poaceae-derived families intermediate to those of the very new and very old. However, the relatively high proportion of genes in Poaceae-derived families with conserved functional domains (63% as compared to 91% in ancient families) suggests that de novo origin accounts for a minority of these families. Indeed, among <i>Oryza</i>-derived loci, the small proportion (~10%) with recognizable domains would have greater potential for future survival as compared to loci from other families. In both Poaceae- and Oryzeae-specific families, genes that do have conserved domains are enriched for defense and stress response functions, suggesting that their rapid evolution was driven by adaptation to varied environmental pressures. Although few putative de novo–originated genes have so far been functionally characterized in plants, the rice <i>OsDR10</i> gene, conserved only in the Oryzeae tribe, is notable in having apparent function in the regulation of pathogen defense responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Xiao, W. et al. A rice gene of de novo origin negatively regulates pathogen-induced defense response. PLoS One 4, e4603 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4194">96</a></sup>.</p><p>TEs make an important contribution to the formation of lineage-specific loci in plants, by exaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Donoghue, M. T., Keshavaiah, C., Swamidatta, S. H. &amp; Spillane, C. Evolutionary origins of Brassicaceae specific genes in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 47 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4201">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wu, D.-D. et al. “Out of pollen” hypothesis for origin of new genes in flowing plants: study from Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 2822–2829 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4204">64</a></sup> or exon capture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Jiang, N., Bao, Z., Zhang, X., Eddy, S. R. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Pack-MULE transposable elements mediate gene evolution in plants. Nature 431, 569–573 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4208">68</a></sup>. Here MDLs accounted for almost 7% of Oryzeae-derived and species-specific loci. We also found that lincRNA genes, which mostly arose in a species-specific manner, are associated with both DNA and LTR-RT-type transposons. Aside from these, we found a clear positional bias of putative new loci over retrotransposon-rich pericentromeric regions, suggesting that LTR-RT activity may contribute to the origination of new genes. Although yet to be confirmed, the observed rapid rates of insertion and excision of LTR-RTs may drive the generation of novel sequences at these sites with chance generation of ORFs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="SanMiguel, P. et al. Nested retrotransposons in the intergenic regions of the maize genome. Science 274, 765–768 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4212">97</a></sup>. An unanticipated finding is that the rate of silent site substitutions was elevated in new gene families as compared to older ones. Elevated synonymous substitutions may be caused by higher background mutation rates in pericentromeric regions as compared to gene-rich regions, as has been found in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Yang, L. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Factors that contribute to variation in evolutionary rate among Arabidopsis genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2359–2369 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4218">98</a></sup>. A second hypothesis is that background mutations accumulate more rapidly owing to suppressed meiotic recombination in pericentromeres. High recombination rates in euchromatin provide opportunities for allelic gene conversion during meiosis, correcting mismatches caused by spontaneous mutation.</p><p>Disease pathogens remain a major threat to rice harvests worldwide, with yearly losses from the most serious disease, blast (<i>Magnaporthe oryzae</i>), having the potential to feed an estimated 60 million people<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Pennisi, E. Armed and dangerous. Science 327, 804–805 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4228">99</a></sup>. Future forecasts may become dire as climate change alters or expands the geographic range of pathogens. Risk-reducing growing techniques and breeding of natural resistance have historically provided the most practical and effective means of control, with chemical fungicides as a last resort in some countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Pooja, K. &amp; Katoch, A. Past, present and future of rice blast management. Plant Sci. Today 1, 165–173 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4232">100</a></sup>. Yet, natural resistance is fragile owing to the rapid evolution of new pathovars that evade host recognition. Stacking of traits from the large array of resistance genes and haplotypes in existing rice germplasm is a realistic approach to build durable resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Fukuoka, S. et al. Gene pyramiding enhances durable blast disease resistance in rice. Sci. Rep. 5, 7773 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4236">101</a></sup>. Our sequencing of seven wild relatives of crop species opens a treasure trove of novel resistance haplotypes and loci to sustain this strategy. The practical utility of our resources is directly demonstrated by our identification of a strong candidate for the long-sought <i>Pi-ta2</i> locus, which in combination with <i>Pi-ta</i> provides broad-specificity resistance to <i>M. oryzae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Bryan, G. T. et al. A single amino acid difference distinguishes resistant and susceptible alleles of the rice blast resistance gene Pi-ta. Plant Cell 12, 2033–2046 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4249">87</a></sup>.</p><p>Sequencing of the first two Asian rice genomes over a decade ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4256">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4259">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92–100 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4262">102</a></sup> greatly accelerated the identification and characterization of genes important to breeders, as well as insights into selective pressures acting on their evolution. The availability of 13 high-quality reference genome assemblies, presented here, now permits exploration of the majority of orthologous loci and genomic regions at will for the AA, BB and FF genome types and <i>L. perrieri</i>. As more wild and cultivated <i>Oryza</i> genomes become available, this resource will become even more powerful.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">The 13-genome data package: sequence, assembly and annotation</h3><p>Detailed descriptions of genome sequencing, assembly, assembly validation and annotation are provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> under the same title.</p><p>Briefly, chromosome-level reference assemblies for two domesticated (<i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> cv. IR 8 (Miracle Rice) and the drought-tolerant <i>aus</i> variety N 22) and seven wild species (<i>O. rufipogon</i>, <i>O. nivara</i>, <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>O. meridionalis</i>, <i>O. punctata</i> and <i>L. perrieri</i>) were generated using either long- or short-read technologies; most of these have extensive scaffold support from long-insert library reads, including BAC-ends, as summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Tab1">1</a>. These assemblies, plus the four additional published assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Genome and comparative transcriptomics of African wild rice Oryza longistaminata provide insights into molecular mechanism of rhizomatousness and self-incompatibility. Mol. Plant 8, 1683–1686 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4325">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. F. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4328">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4328_1">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4331">30</a></sup>, were annotated using a uniform annotation pipeline to minimize biases associated with different methods for gene and repeat identification (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Phylogenetic inference</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Data preparation</h4><p>For each chromosome, we used the BLAST-Overlap-Synteny (BOS) filtering protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zwickl, D. J., Stein, J. C., Wing, R. A., Ware, D. &amp; Sanderson, M. J. Disentangling methodological and biological sources of gene tree discordance on Oryza (Poaceae) chromosome 3. Syst. Biol. 63, 645–659 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4350">34</a></sup> to identify clusters of single-copy orthologous loci. Only clusters containing a single sequence from each genome were used in further analyses (note: the IR 8 and N 22 genomes were not used in this analysis as they did not provide any meaningful phylogenetic signal beyond the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence). The coding regions of each locus were aligned at the nucleotide level using PRANK v.140110<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Löytynoja, A. &amp; Goldman, N. An algorithm for progressive multiple alignment of sequences with insertions. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 10557–10562 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4354">105</a></sup> with the -F setting. For each chromosome, alignments containing sequences from all 11 genomes were concatenated to create 12 ‘supermatrix’ alignments.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Maximum-likelihood tree inference</h4><p>Maximum-likelihood phylogenies were inferred for individual locus alignments and supermatrices using GARLI version 2.01<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Zwickl, D. J. Genetic Algorithm Approaches for the Phylogenetic Analysis of Large Biological Sequence Datasets under the Maximum Likelihood Criterion. PhD thesis, Univ. Texas, Austin (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4366">106</a></sup> (default settings except genthreshfortopoterm = 5000 and startoptprec = 0.01). For each individual gene alignment, the best fitting substitution model was chosen using ModelTest v3.7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Posada, D. &amp; Crandall, K. A. MODELTEST: testing the model of DNA substitution. Bioinformatics 14, 817–818 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4370">107</a></sup> with the Akaike information criterion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Hirotugu, A. A new look at the statistical model identification. IEEE Trans. Automat. Contr. 19, 716–723 (1974)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4374">108</a></sup>. Supermatrix analyses were run using a partitioned likelihood substitution model that allowed each locus an independent relative substitution rate. A single general time-reversible nucleotide substitution model with gamma-distributed rate heterogeneity and an estimated proportion of invariable sites was shared by all loci within each supermatrix. Confidence estimates were obtained from 400 bootstrap search replicates computed on each supermatrix. In addition, 200 bootstrap tree searches were performed on each individual locus and used as input to species tree analyses. During bootstrap analyses, substitution model parameter values were fixed at the values estimated during the maximum-likelihood tree search, for computational efficiency. Branches with negligible maximum-likelihood branch length estimates (1 × 10<sup>−8</sup>) were collapsed, creating polytomies.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Species tree analyses</h4><p>The MP-EST program v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Liu, L., Yu, L. &amp; Edwards, S. V. A maximum pseudo-likelihood approach for estimating species trees under the coalescent model. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 302 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4388">109</a></sup> was used to infer species trees from collections of gene trees using a multispecies coalescent model. Analyses were run independently for each chromosome. Polytomies in locus trees were first randomly resolved with zero-length branches. To account for uncertainty in the inference of gene trees for each locus and in the arbitrary resolution of polytomies, MP-EST was run multiple times using gene trees selected from bootstrap samples. For each chromosome, one tree was randomly selected for each locus from the bootstrap samples, and this collection of trees was used as input to MP-EST. This procedure was repeated 400 times. MP-EST support values for species tree branches were calculated as the proportion of replicates returning a species tree containing that branch.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Oryza divergence time estimation</h4><p>Divergence times within <i>Oryza</i> were estimated using the supermatrix phylogenies inferred for each chromosome. The phylogenies were rooted using the outgroup <i>L. perrieri</i>, which was subsequently pruned from the trees. These pruned trees, with maximum-likelihood branch length estimates obtained from GARLI, were used as input to PATHd8 v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Britton, T., Anderson, C. L., Jacquet, D., Lundqvist, S. &amp; Bremer, K. Estimating divergence times in large phylogenetic trees. Syst. Biol. 56, 741–752 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4406">110</a></sup>. PATHd8 smooths substitution rates across branches to transform a tree with branch lengths measured as substitutions per site to an ultrametric tree with relative node ages. To convert the relative ages to absolute ages, the age of the crown group of <i>Oryza</i> (the root of the pruned tree) was fixed using a calibration consistent with several recent studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Long-range and targeted ectopic recombination between the two homeologous chromosomes 11 and 12 in Oryza species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 3139–3150 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4413">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Tang, L. et al. Phylogeny and biogeography of the rice tribe (Oryzeae): evidence from combined analysis of 20 chloroplast fragments. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 54, 266–277 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4416">111</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Zou, X. H. et al. Analysis of 142 genes resolves the rapid diversification of the rice genus. Genome Biol. 9, R49 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4419">112</a></sup> (15 million years).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec19">Introgression among AA genomes</h4><p>We sought evidence of introgression within the AA-genome species to understand further some of the gene tree discordance observed in the chromosome-level analyses. We used the <i>D</i> statistic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Green, R. E. et al. A draft sequence of the Neandertal genome. Science 328, 710–722 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4435">113</a></sup>, which examines site patterns (so-called ABBA/ABAB patterns) in genome alignments for a specified four-taxon tree. A significant departure from zero of the difference in numbers of the two kinds of ‘introgression’ sites was tested with a block-jackknife procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Durand, E. Y., Patterson, N., Reich, D. &amp; Slatkin, M. Testing for ancient admixture between closely related populations. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2239–2252 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4439">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Busing, F., Meijer, E. &amp; Leeden, R. Delete-m Jackknife for Unequal m. Stat. Comput. 9, 3–8 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4442">114</a></sup>, which is aimed at overcoming autocorrelations within the sequence. We focused on the case that seemed problematic in gene/species tree construction: the position of <i>O. glumaepatula</i>. We used the tree (<i>O. punctata</i>, (<i>O. glumaepatula</i>, (<i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. rufipogon</i>))) to see whether there was evidence that <i>O. glumaepatula</i> has undergone introgression with either the African <i>O. barthii</i> or Asian <i>O. rufipogon</i>. As a ‘control’, we also considered the tree (<i>O. punctata</i>, (<i>O. meridionalis</i>, (<i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. rufipogon</i>))), which replaces the problematic <i>O. glumaepatula</i> with another taxon in the AA clade, <i>O. meridionalis</i>, which is more distantly related to the Asian/African pair. Small alignment blocks sampled across the sequences of each chromosome were assembled using the program hakmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Sanderson, M. J., Nicolae, M. &amp; McMahon, M. M. Homology-aware phylogenomics at gigabase scales. Syst. Biol. 66, 590–603 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4491">115</a></sup>, which identifies exactly matching syntenic single-copy <i>k</i>-mers (<i>k</i> = 32) found in all four taxa, extended by an ungapped 25-nt region up- and downstream of each <i>k</i>-mer. Typically, the final datasets included 10–15% of the original sequence data after this culling. <i>D</i> statistics were computed with a Perl script (available upon request), and block-jackknife estimates of the standard error of <i>D</i> were obtained using <i>m</i> = 20 blocks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Concerted evolution in chromosomes 11 and 12</h3><p>Whole-chromosome alignments of complete sequences of chromosomes 11 and 12 from <i>O. sativa</i> (Nipponbare RefSeq sequence), <i>O. rufipogon</i>, <i>O. glaberrima</i>, <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. punctata</i>, <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>L. perrieri</i> were carried out using a modification of the method described in Jacquemin et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Long-range and targeted ectopic recombination between the two homeologous chromosomes 11 and 12 in Oryza species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 3139–3150 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4545">14</a></sup> (note: the IR 8 and N 22 genomes were not used in this analysis as they did not provide any meaningful phylogenetic signal beyond the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence). Initial alignment was made with Mugsy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Angiuoli, S. V. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Mugsy: fast multiple alignment of closely related whole genomes. Bioinformatics 27, 334–342 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4549">116</a></sup>, using the parameters -d 100 -c 200 -nucmeropts -l 20, and alignments were refined with Mugsy itself. All subsequent analyses were encapsulated by in-house scripts. Multiple-sequence alignments (300 bp) were generated directly from the MAF file and converted into a nexus format. Sequences with a majority of undefined bases or that were &lt; 50 bp in length were eliminated. Indels were coded with Indelcoder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Ogden, T. H. &amp; Rosenberg, M. S. How should gaps be treated in parsimony? A comparison of approaches using simulation. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 42, 817–826 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4553">117</a></sup> as described, and trees were generated with MRBAYES<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4557">118</a></sup> using the following parameters: Nst = 2 and Rates = Invgamma. The data were partitioned according to type: DNA and gap binary information. For the gap binary data partition, we selected the option coding = variable. The four chains were run for 100,000 generations, sampling trees every 100 generations, with the first 25% of trees sampled discarded as burn-in. Posterior probabilities were calculated from the remaining samples. Intra- and interspecies distances were calculated from the output consensus file. Trees containing sequences from at least five species were retained for analysis. Phylogenetic (<i>n</i> = 5,782) trees were retained and used for calculation of intra- and interspecies Bayesian distances using BioPerl modules. For AA-genome analysis, sequences of chromosomes 11 and 12 from <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>O. rufipogon</i>, <i>O. glaberrima</i>, <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. nivara</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>O. meridionalis</i> and <i>O. punctata</i> were used. Divergence dates were calculated from the median nucleotide distance values as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jacquemin, J. et al. Long-range and targeted ectopic recombination between the two homeologous chromosomes 11 and 12 in Oryza species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 3139–3150 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4590">14</a></sup>. Dates of divergence between species calculated by this method are consistent with published estimates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Synteny maps and screen for chromosomal inversions</h3><p>Syntenic relationships between orthologous genes were mapped for all 78 pairwise combinations of the 13 reference assemblies. We used DAGchainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4602">119</a></sup> to identify collinear gene pairs within syntenic blocks, with parameters requiring neighboring genes to be no more than ten genes apart and a minimum chain length of five collinear genes. As this method is strict, we additionally identified ‘in-range’ syntenies of orthologs that mapped no more than five genes distant from the expected collinear position<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Youens-Clark, K. et al. Gramene database in 2010: updates and extensions. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D1085–D1094 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4606">120</a></sup>. Clustered sets of syntenic genes encompassing all species were identified by single-linkage clustering over the pairwise relationships. As a second approach, we conducted pairwise whole-genome alignments using LASTZ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Harris, R. S. Improved Pairwise Alignment of Genomic DNA. PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4610">121</a></sup> and applied a suite of programs to filter maximum-scoring chains and identify non-overlapping ‘nets’ corresponding to regions of synteny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Kent, W. J., Baertsch, R., Hinrichs, A., Miller, W. &amp; Haussler, D. Evolution’s cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 100, 11484–11489 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4614">122</a></sup>.</p><p>To screen for chromosomal inversions, we first identified in each species the set of syntenic blocks (consisting of five or more collinear orthologs) having reverse orientation relative to the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence (note: the IR 8 and N 22 genomes were not used in this analysis as they did not provide any meaningful phylogenetic signal beyond the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence). Identified blocks were further screened to remove blocks associated with duplication or movement, by ensuring that the immediate upstream and downstream syntenic blocks were contiguous and in the forward orientation in both genomes. To increase confidence in our identification, we next screened for putative inversion events shared by two or more species. Putative inversions that overlapped with respect to IRGSP-1.0 coordinates were identified using BEDTools v2.17.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4621">123</a></sup>. Shared events were also required to have LASTZ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Harris, R. S. Improved Pairwise Alignment of Genomic DNA. PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4625">121</a></sup> alignment breakpoints within 20 kb of one another in the IRGSP reference. The set of candidate inversion events was further refined to include only those that could be logically placed at a discrete internal branch of the species tree. Thus, the final set of inversion events was identified as synteny blocks found in reverse orientation in all outgroup species and in the forward orientation in all in-group species. A synteny map of IRGSP-1.0 with <i>B. distachyon</i> (built in the same manner as described above) was used as an additional outgroup to confirm the ancestral state of the region.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">LTR–retrotransposon deletion rate</h3><p>To estimate the deletion rate of LTR-RTs in rice, we first identified loci of the genomes that were absent from <i>O. meridionalis</i> but partly conserved in the remaining eight sequenced AA genomes and that contained deleted LTR-RTs. This was achieved in the following way. (1) All AA genomes were analyzed with RepeatMasker (see URLs) using a database of only full-length LTR-RTs to identify regions containing LTR-RT sequences. (2) To identify orthologous regions between the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4644">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Toh, H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief. Bioinform. 9, 286–298 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4647">126</a></sup> and all the other AA genomes (query), sequences were aligned with NUCmer from the MUMmer package (version 3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4651">125</a></sup> using the Nipponbare RefSeq sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4655">29</a></sup> (IRGSP1.0 assembly) as the reference: nucmer -g 500 &lt;reference&gt;  &lt;query&gt;, default for all other options. The NUCmer outputs were then parsed with the utility show-coords from the same package: show-coords -r -g -I 70 -T -H &lt;nucmer output&gt;. The -g option was used to secure the orthologous relationship between the conserved blocks, thereby allowing the identification of indels by the break between two adjacent alignments. These analyses allowed the identification of conserved blocks between <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> and each of the other AA genomes. Moreover, as mentioned above, the show-coords output from the <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i>–<i>O. meridionalis</i> comparison led to the identification of indels between the two genomes (regions of <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> that were absent from <i>O. meridionalis</i> at orthologous positions). (3) Starting from the show-coords output, custom Perl scripts were used to identify regions that were common between <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>japonica</i> and one to five of the seven remaining AA genomes (<i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i>, <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. glaberrima</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>O. nivara</i>, <i>O. rufipogon</i> and <i>O. meridionalis</i>) and that overlapped regions identified as LTR-RT parts by the RepeatMasker analysis (note: all data extracted from <i>O. sativa</i> vg. <i>indica</i> were identical to the IR 8 RefSeq data and thus were not used in this analysis) and to extract the sequences corresponding to these orthologous regions in all AA genomes, including 500 bp before and after the break position in <i>O. meridionalis</i>, to create multifasta files for each selected locus.</p><p>The whole set of sequences from one multifasta file were aligned using MAFFT v6.940b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Toh, H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief. Bioinform. 9, 286–298 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0040-0#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d4418e4729">126</a></sup> as follows: mafft -op 5 -ep 0 &lt; multifasta file &gt; . Increasing the opening penalty (-op) as compared to the default options allowed identification of the deleted parts from each sequence.
